id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery">Space Shuttle Discovery</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" <i>Psyche</i> missions</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="375" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg/220px-Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg/330px-Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Properties_of_regolith_on_Eros.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asteroid Eros regolith, as viewed by Discovery\'s NEAR Shoemaker mission</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <b>Discovery Program</b> is a series of lower-cost (as compared to <a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">New Frontiers</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">Flagship</a> Programs), highly focused American scientific <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space missions</a> that are exploring the Solar System. It was founded in 1992 to implement then-NASA Administrator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_S._Goldin" title="Daniel S. Goldin">Daniel S. Goldin</a>\'s vision of "faster, better, cheaper" planetary missions. Discovery missions differ from traditional NASA missions where targets and objectives are pre-specified. Instead, these cost-capped missions are proposed and led by a scientist called the <a href="/wiki/Principal_investigator" title="Principal investigator">Principal Investigator</a> (PI). Proposing teams may include people from industry, small businesses, government laboratories, and universities. Proposals are selected through a competitive peer review process. All of the completed Discovery missions are accomplishing ground-breaking science and adding significantly to the body of knowledge about the Solar System.\n</p><p>NASA also accepts proposals for competitively selected Discovery Program Missions of Opportunity. This provides opportunities to participate in non-NASA missions by providing funding for a science instrument or hardware components of a science instrument or to re-purpose an existing NASA spacecraft. These opportunities are currently offered through NASA\'s Stand Alone Mission of Opportunity program.\n</p><p>On January 4, 2017 NASA announced that the next Discovery mission selection included the double-selection of \'\'<i><a href="/wiki/Lucy_(spacecraft)" title="Lucy (spacecraft)">Lucy</a></i>\'\', to visit several <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Trojans</a>, and \'\'<i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_(spacecraft)" title="Psyche (spacecraft)">Psyche</a></i>\'\', to visit the metal asteroid <a href="/wiki/16_Psyche" title="16 Psyche">Psyche</a>, as the thirteenth and fourteenth Discovery missions, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasataps_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasataps-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1989, the Solar System Exploration Division (SSED) at NASA Headquarters initiated a series of workshops to define a new strategy for exploration through the year 2000. The panels included a Small Mission Program Group (SMPG) that was chartered to devise a rationale for missions that would be low cost and allow focused scientific questions to be addressed in a relatively short time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-History-3">[3]</a></sup> A fast-paced study for a potential mission was requested and funding arrangements were made in 1990. The new program was called \'Discovery\' and the panel assessed a number of concepts that could be implemented as low-cost programs, with \'<a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">Near Earth Asteroid Rendezvous</a>\' (<a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR</a>) as the first mission to be implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-History-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On February 17, 1996, NEAR became the first mission to launch in the Discovery Program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-History-3">[3]</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder" title="Mars Pathfinder">Mars Pathfinder</a> launched on December 4, 1996, demonstrated a number of innovative, economical, and highly effective approaches to spacecraft and planetary mission design such as the inflated <a href="/wiki/Airbag#Spacecraft_airbag_landing_systems" title="Airbag">airbags</a> that allowed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sojourner_rover" title="Sojourner rover"><i>Sojourner</i> rover</a> to endure the landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-History-3">[3]</a></sup> Note that the two <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rovers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opportunity_rover" title="Opportunity rover"><i>Opportunity</i></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_rover" title="Spirit rover"><i>Spirit</i></a>, were not a part of the Discovery Program, although they did re-use the overall landing system of Mars Pathfinder. Also, Phoenix and MAVEN were in the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Scout_Program" title="Mars Scout Program">Mars Scout Program</a> and not the Discovery Program.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_timeline">Mission timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" class="mw-headline" id="Missions">Missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standalone_missions">Standalone missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Standalone missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="margin:1em auto;">\n<caption>Standalone Discovery Program missions\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col">&#8470;\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Name\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Targets\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Launch date\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Rocket\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Launch mass\n</th>\n<th scope="col">First science\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Status\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Principal investigator\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Cost <br/>million USD\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">1\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR Shoemaker</a>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a></b> (lander), <a href="/wiki/253_Mathilde" title="253 Mathilde">253 Mathilde</a>\n</td>\n<td>February 17, 1996\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925-8\n</td>\n<td>800&#160;kg<br/>(1,800&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>June 1997\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2001\n</td>\n<td>Andrew Cheng <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">APL</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>224<br/>(2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i><b>N</b>ear <b>E</b>arth <b>A</b>steroid <b>R</b>endezvous &#8211; Shoemaker</i> (named after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Shoemaker" title="Eugene Shoemaker">Eugene Shoemaker</a>) performed a flyby of <a href="/wiki/253_Mathilde" title="253 Mathilde">253 Mathilde</a> on 27 June 1997, and an Earth flyby in 1998. It flew by <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a> once in 1998, before a second approach allowed it to enter orbit around Eros of February 14, 2000. After a year of orbital observations, the spacecraft was landed on the asteroid on February 2001, and surprisingly continued to function successfully touched down softly at under 2&#160;m/s, becoming the first probe to soft-land on an asteroid. The probe continued to emit signals until February 28, 2001, and the final attempt to communicate with the spacecraft was on December 10, 2002.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">2\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder" title="Mars Pathfinder">Mars Pathfinder</a>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></b> (rover)\n</td>\n<td>December 4, 1996\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925\n</td>\n<td>264&#160;kg<br/>(582&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>July 4, 1997\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 1998\n</td>\n<td>Joseph Boyce<br/> (<a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a>)\n</td>\n<td>265<br/>(1998)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">It was launched a month after the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a>. After entering the Martian atmosphere, the hypersonic capsule deployed complex landing system including a parachute and an airbag to hit the surface at 14&#160;m/s. The lander deployed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sojourner_rover" title="Sojourner rover"><i>Sojourner</i> rover</a> (10.5&#160;kg) on the surface on July 4, 1997 on Mars\'s <a href="/wiki/Ares_Vallis" title="Ares Vallis">Ares Vallis</a> thus becoming the first rover to operate outside the Earth-Moon system. It carried a series of scientific instruments to analyze the Martian atmosphere, climate, geology and the composition of its rocks and soil. It completed its primary and extended mission and after over 80 days, the last signal was sent on September 27, 1997. Mission was terminated on March 10, 1998.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">3\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">Lunar Prospector</a>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>January 7, 1998\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Athena_II" title="Athena II">Athena II</a> <br/><span class="nowrap">[Star-3700S]</span>\n</td>\n<td>296&#160;kg<br/>(653&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>January 16, 1998\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 1999\n</td>\n<td>Alan Binder <br/>(<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Research_Institute&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lunar Research Institute (page does not exist)">LRI</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>63<br/>(1998)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">A Moon orbiter to characterize the lunar mineralogy, including polar ice deposits, measurements of magnetic and gravity fields, and study of lunar outgassing events. After preliminary mappings, it achieved the targeted primary Lunar orbit on January 16. Primary mission in this orbit lasted one year until January 28, 1999, followed up by a half-year extended mission in a lower orbit for higher resolution. On July 31, 1999 it deliberately impacted into the <a href="/wiki/Shoemaker_(lunar_crater)" title="Shoemaker (lunar crater)">Shoemaker</a> crater near the Lunar South pole in hope to produce water vapor plumes that would be observable from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">4\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/81P/Wild" title="81P/Wild">81P/Wild</a></b> (sample collect), <a href="/wiki/5535_Annefrank" title="5535 Annefrank">5535 Annefrank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a>\n</td>\n<td>February 7, 1999\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7426-9.5\n</td>\n<td>391&#160;kg<br/>(862&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>November 2, 2002\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2011\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donald_Brownlee" title="Donald Brownlee">Donald Brownlee</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">UW</a>)\n</td>\n<td>200<br/>(2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">Mission to collect interstellar dust and dust particles from the nucleus of comet <a href="/wiki/81P/Wild" title="81P/Wild">81P/Wild</a> for study on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. After a flyby of Earth and then of asteroid <a href="/wiki/5535_Annefrank" title="5535 Annefrank">5535 Annefrank</a> in November 2002. After a flyby of comet Wild 2 in January 2004, during which the Sample Collection plate collected dust grain samples from the <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>. Later, it separated from its sample-return capsule, which returned to Earth on January 15, 2006. This capsule is on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in Washington D.C. Scientists worldwide are studying the comet dust samples while citizen scientists are finding interstellar dust bits through the <a href="/wiki/Stardust@home" title="Stardust@home">Stardust@home</a> project, and in 2014, scientists announced the identification of possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">interstellar dust</a> particles. Meanwhile, the spacecraft averted reentry, and was diverted for a flyby of <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> comet, as part of <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)#New_Exploration_of_Tempel_1_(NExT)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust-NExT</a> extension, to observe the crater left by <i>Deep Impact</i>. \'Stardust<i> did a final burn to deplete its remaining fuel on March 24, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">5\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">Solar wind</a></b> (collect at Sun&#8211;Earth L<sub>1</sub>)\n</td>\n<td>August 8, 2001\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7326\n</td>\n<td>494&#160;kg<br/>(1,089&#160;lb)(dry)\n</td>\n<td>December 3, 2001\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2004\n</td>\n<td>Donald Burnett <br/>(<a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>209M<br/>(2004)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">Mission to collect <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> charged particles for analysis on Earth. After reaching L<sub>1</sub> orbit on November 16, 2001, it collected solar wind for 850 days between 2001&#8211;2004. It left <a href="/wiki/Lissajous_orbit" title="Lissajous orbit">Lissajous orbit</a> and began its return to Earth on April 22, 2004, but on September 8, 2004, the sample-return capsule\'s parachute failed to deploy, and the capsule crashed into the Utah desert. However, solar wind samples were salvaged and are available for study. Despite the hard landing, <i>Genesis</i> has met or anticipates meeting all its baseline science objectives.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">6\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/CONTOUR" title="CONTOUR">CONTOUR</a>\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Comet_Encke" title="Comet Encke">Encke</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schwassmann-Wachmann-3" title="Schwassmann-Wachmann-3">Schwassmann-Wachmann-3</a>\n</td>\n<td>July 3, 2002\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> 7425 <br/>[Star-30BP]\n</td>\n<td>398&#160;kg<br/>(877&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>&#8212;\n</td>\n<td class="table-failure" style="background: #ffbbbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Disintegrated <br/>after launch\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Veverka" title="Joseph Veverka">Joseph Veverka</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>154<br/> (1997)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i><b>Co</b>met <b>N</b>ucleus <b>Tour</b></i> was a failed mission to visit and study comets. On August 15, 2002, after a planned maneuver that was intended to propel it out of Earth orbit and into its comet-chasing solar orbit, the spacecraft was lost. The investigation board concluded the probable cause was structural failure of the spacecraft due to plume heating during the Star-30 solid-rocket motor burn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-History-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Subsequent investigation revealed that it broke into at least three pieces, the cause likely being structural failure during the rocket motor burn that was to push it from Earth orbit into a solar orbit.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">7\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a></b>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>\n</td>\n<td>August 3, 2004\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925H-9.5\n</td>\n<td>1,108&#160;kg<br/>(2,443&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>August 2005\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2015\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Sean_Solomon" title="Sean Solomon">Sean Solomon</a><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">APL</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2004_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2004-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>450<br/>(2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i><b>Me</b>rcury <b>S</b>urface, <b>S</b>pace <b>E</b>nvironment, <b>Ge</b>ochemistry and <b>R</b>anging</i> conducted the first orbital study of Mercury. Its science goals were to provide the first images of the entire planet and collect detailed information on the composition and structure of Mercury\'s crust, its geologic history, the nature of its thin atmosphere and active magnetosphere, and the makeup of its core and polar materials. It was only the second spacecraft to flyby Mercury, after <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a> in 1975. After one Earth flyby, two of Venus and three of Mercury, it finally entered orbit around Mercury on March 18, 2011. The primary science mission began on April 4, 2011 and lasted until March 17, 2012. It achieved 100% mapping of Mercury on March 6, 2013, and completed its first year-long extended mission on March 17, 2013. After two extension missions, the spacecraft ran out of propellant and was deorbited on April 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2004_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2004-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">8\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a></b> (impactor), <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">103P/Hartley</a>\n</td>\n<td>January 12, 2005\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925\n</td>\n<td>650&#160;kg<br/>(1,430&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>April 25, 2005\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2013\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Michael_A%27Hearn" title="Michael A\'Hearn">Michael A\'Hearn</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_College_Park" title="University of Maryland, College Park">UMD</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2005_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2005-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>330<br/>(2005)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">The spacecraft released a 350&#160;kg impactor into the path of comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a> on July 3, 2005. The impact occurred on July 4, 2005 releasing an energy equivalent of 4.7 tons of <a href="/wiki/TNT" title="TNT">TNT</a>. Plume of the impact were observed with several space-based observatories. The 2007 Stardust spacecraft NExT mission determined the resulting crater\'s diameter to be 150 meters (490&#160;ft). After the successful completion of its mission, it was put in hibernation and then reactivated for a new mission designated <a href="/wiki/EPOXI" title="EPOXI">EPOXI</a>.  On November 4, 2010 it performed a flyby of comet <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">Hartley 2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2005_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2005-20">[20]</a></sup>  In 2012 it performed long-distance observations of comet Garradd <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=C/2009_P1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="C/2009 P1 (page does not exist)">C/2009 P1</a>, and in 2013 of <a href="/wiki/Comet_ISON" title="Comet ISON">Comet ISON</a>. Contact was lost in August 2013, later attributed to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y2K" title="Y2K">Y2K</a>-like error.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">9\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a>, <b><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>September 27, 2007\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925H\n</td>\n<td>1,218&#160;kg<br/>(2,685&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>May 3, 2011\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2018\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Christopher_T._Russell" title="Christopher T. Russell">Christopher T. Russell</a> (<a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">UCLA</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>472<br/> (2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i>Dawn</i> is the first spacecraft to orbit two extraterrestrial bodies, the two most massive objects of the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanet</a> <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>.  This was made possible by employing highly efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_electric" title="Solar electric">solar electric</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_thrusters" title="Ion thrusters">ion thrusters</a>, with just 425&#160;kg of <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> for the entire mission after escaping Earth.  After a 2009 Mars flyby, it entered orbit around Vesta on July 16, 2011.  It entered its lowest Vesta orbit on December 8, 2011, and after a year-long Vesta mission, it left its orbit on September 5, 2012.  It entered Ceres\' orbit on March 6, 2015 thus becoming the first spacecraft to visit a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>. It began its lowest orbit on December 16, 2015, and in June 2016 it was approved for an extended mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewSci2012_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewSci2012-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn_timetable_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawn_timetable-25">[25]</a></sup>  On October 19, 2017, NASA announced that the mission would be extended until its hydrazine fuel runs out, possibly in the second half of 2018.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">10\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler</a></i>\n</th>\n<td>transiting <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> survey\n</td>\n<td>March 7, 2009\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7925-10L\n</td>\n<td>1,052&#160;kg<br/>(2,319&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>May 12, 2009\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2018\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Borucki" title="William Borucki">William Borucki</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">NASA Ames</a>)\n</td>\n<td>640<br/>(2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_observatory" title="Space observatory">space observatory</a> named after <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> in an heliocentric, Earth-trailing orbit tasked to explore the structure and diversity of <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> systems, with a special emphasis on the detection of Earth-size planets in orbit around stars outside our Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> It announced its first exoplanet discoveries in January 2010. Initially planned for 3.5 years, it is still functional after 9 years, including a  <i>K2</i> "Second Light" extension mission.  By 2015, the spacecraft had detected over 2,300 confirmed planets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150106_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150106-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circumbinary_planet" title="Circumbinary planet">circumbinary planets</a>, and planets located in the <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">circumstellar habitable zones</a> of their host stars. In addition, <i>Kepler</i> has detected over 3,600 unconfirmed planet candidates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wall-2013_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-wall-2013-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keplersite_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-keplersite-31">[31]</a></sup> and over 2,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipsing_binary_star" title="Eclipsing binary star">eclipsing binary stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keplersite_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-keplersite-31">[31]</a></sup> The telescope retired on October 30, 2018 after finally running out of fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">11\n</th>\n<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Interior_Laboratory" title="Gravity Recovery and Interior Laboratory">GRAIL</a>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>September 10, 2011\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a><br/> 7920H-10C\n</td>\n<td>307&#160;kg<br/>(677&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>Mar 7, 2012\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;">Completed in 2012\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Maria_Zuber" title="Maria Zuber">Maria Zuber</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>)\n</td>\n<td>496 <br/>(2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i><b>G</b>ravity <b>R</b>ecovery <b>a</b>nd <b>I</b>nterior <b>L</b>aboratory</i> provided higher-quality <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> mapping of the Moon to determine its interior structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaunchCBS_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaunchCBS-34">[34]</a></sup> The two small spacecrafts <i>GRAIL A (Ebb)</i> and <i>GRAIL B (Flow)</i> separated soon after the launch and entered Lunar orbits on December 31, 2011 and January 1, 2012, respectively. The primary scientific phase was achieved in May 2012. After the extended mission phase, the two spacecrafts impacted the Moon on December 17, 2012. MoonKAM (Moon Knowledge Acquired by Middle school students) was an education related sub-program and instrument of this mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">12\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/InSight" title="InSight">InSight</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></b> (lander)\n</td>\n<td>May 5, 2018\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a><br/> (401)\n</td>\n<td>721&#160;kg<br/>(1,590&#160;lb)\n</td>\n<td>November 2018\n</td>\n<td class="table-success" style="background: #bbffbb; color: black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;"><b>Operational</b>\n</td>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=W._Bruce_Banerdt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="W. Bruce Banerdt (page does not exist)">W. Bruce Banerdt</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a>)\n</td>\n<td>830<br/>(2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i><b>I</b>nterior Exploration using <b>S</b>eismic <b>I</b>nvestigations, <b>G</b>eodesy and <b>H</b>eat <b>T</b>ransport</i> is a 358&#160;kg lander reusing technology from the Mars <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenix_lander" title="Phoenix lander">Phoenix lander</a>.  It is intended to study the interior structure and composition of Mars and advance understanding of the formation and evolution of terrestrial planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Its launch was delayed from 2016 to May 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delay_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-delay-38">[38]</a></sup>  The lander touched down successfully on November 26, 2018, at a site about 600&#160;km (370&#160;mi) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curiosity_rover" title="Curiosity rover">Curiosity rover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">13\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Lucy_(spacecraft)" title="Lucy (spacecraft)">Lucy</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>October 2021\n</td>\n<td>TBA\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>2025\n</td>\n<td><center>In development</center>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Harold_F._Levison" title="Harold F. Levison">Harold F. Levison</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">SwRI</a>)\n</td>\n<td>450 + launch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceflightnow1_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-spaceflightnow1-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">Named after the hominin <a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">Lucy</a>, it will tour six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroids" title="Trojan asteroids">Trojan asteroids</a> in order to better understand the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of the Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jobs_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-jobs-41">[41]</a></sup> The launch is planned for 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> It will arrive at Jupiter\'s L4 Trojan cloud in 2027 to visit <a href="/wiki/3548_Eurybates" title="3548 Eurybates">3548 Eurybates</a>, <a href="/wiki/15094_Polymele" title="15094 Polymele">15094 Polymele</a>, <a href="/wiki/11351_Leucus" title="11351 Leucus">11351 Leucus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/21900_Orus" title="21900 Orus">21900 Orus</a>.  After an Earth flyby, <i>Lucy</i> will arrive at the L5 Trojan cloud (trails behind Jupiter) to visit the <a href="/wiki/617_Patroclus" title="617 Patroclus">617 Patroclus</a>&#8722;Menoetius binary in 2033. It will also fly by the inner main-belt asteroid <a href="/wiki/52246_Donaldjohanson" title="52246 Donaldjohanson">52246 Donaldjohanson</a> in 2025.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th rowspan="2" scope="row" style="text-align:center;">14\n</th>\n<th><i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_(spacecraft)" title="Psyche (spacecraft)">Psyche</a></i>\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/16_Psyche" title="16 Psyche">16 Psyche</a></b>\n</td>\n<td>2022\n</td>\n<td>TBA\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>2026\n</td>\n<td><center>In development</center>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lindy_Elkins-Tanton" title="Lindy Elkins-Tanton">Lindy Elkins-Tanton</a> <br/>(<a href="/wiki/Arizona_State_University" title="Arizona State University">ASU</a>)\n</td>\n<td>450 + launch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceflightnow1_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-spaceflightnow1-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10"><i>Psyche</i> orbiter will travel to and study the asteroid <a href="/wiki/16_Psyche" title="16 Psyche">16 Psyche</a>, the most massive metallic asteroid in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, thought to be the exposed iron core of a protoplanet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hou.usra.edu_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-hou.usra.edu-43">[43]</a></sup> The launch is planned for 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> It will carry an imager, a magnetometer, and a gamma-ray spectrometer.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions_of_opportunity">Missions of opportunity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Missions of opportunity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>This provides opportunities to participate in non-NASA missions by providing funding for a science instrument or hardware components of a science instrument, or specific extended mission for spacecraft that may different from its original purpose. Some examples include: ASPERA-3, M3, EPOXI, NExT, and Strofio.\n</p>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express#Mars_Express_instruments" title="Mars Express">ASPERA-3</a></b>, an instrument designed to study the interaction between the solar wind and the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a>, is flying on board the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a> orbiter. Launched in June 2003, it has been orbiting Mars since Dec. 2003. The Principal Investigator is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=David_Winningham&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="David Winningham (page does not exist)">David Winningham</a> of <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a>.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Moon_Mineralogy_Mapper" title="Moon Mineralogy Mapper">Moon Mineralogy Mapper</a></b> (M3) is a NASA-designed instrument placed on board the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">ISRO</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan</a> orbiter selected in February, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Launched in 2008, it was designed to explore the Moon\'s mineral composition at high resolution. M3\'s detection of water on the Moon was announced in Sept. 2009, one month after the mission ended. The Principal Investigator was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carle_Pieters" title="Carle Pieters">Carle Pieters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/EPOXI" title="EPOXI">Extrasolar Planet Observation and Deep Impact Extended Investigation</a></b> (EPOXI) was selected in July, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup> It was a series of two new missions for the existing <i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i> probe following its success at Tempel 1:\n<ul><li>The <b>Extrasolar Planet Observations and Characterization</b> (EPOCh) mission used the Deep Impact high-resolution camera in 2008 to better characterize known giant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planets" title="Extrasolar planets">extrasolar planets</a> orbiting other stars and to search for additional planets in the same system. The Principal Investigator was <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=L._Drake_Deming&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="L. Drake Deming (page does not exist)">L. Drake Deming</a> of NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a>. <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hartley_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1171" data-file-width="1683" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/220px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/330px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Comet_Hartley_2.jpg/440px-Comet_Hartley_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comet_Hartley_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nucleus of Comet Hartley 2</div></div></div></li>\n<li>The <b>Deep Impact eXtended Investigation of Comets</b> (DIXI) mission used the Deep Impact mission\'s spacecraft for a flyby mission to a second comet, <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">Hartley 2</a>. The goal was to take pictures of its nucleus to increase our understanding of the diversity of comets. The flyby of Hartley 2 was successful with closest approach occurring on November 4, 2010. Michael A\'Hearn of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland" title="University of Maryland">University of Maryland</a> was the Principal Investigator.</li></ul></li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)#Secondary_mission:_exploration_of_Tempel_1_(NExT)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">New Exploration of Tempel 1</a></b> (NExT) was selected in July, 2007 together with the EPOXI extension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup> It was a new mission for the <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></i> spacecraft to fly by comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9P/Tempel" title="9P/Tempel">Tempel 1</a> in 2011 and observe changes since the Deep Impact mission visited it in July 2005. Later in 2005, Tempel 1 made its closest approach to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, possibly changing the surface of the comet. The flyby was completed successfully on February 15, 2011. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Veverka" title="Joseph Veverka">Joseph Veverka</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> is the Principal Investigator.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/BepiColombo#Mercury_Planetary_Orbiter" title="BepiColombo">Strofio</a></b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> is a unique <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometer</a> that is part of the SERENA instrument package that will fly on board the European Space Agency\'s BepiColombo/Mercury Planetary Orbiter spacecraft. Strofio will study the atoms and molecules that compose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_atmosphere" title="Mercury\'s atmosphere">Mercury\'s atmosphere</a> to reveal the composition of the planet\'s surface. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stefano_Livi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stefano Livi (page does not exist)">Stefano Livi</a> of Southwest Research Institute is the Principal Investigator.</li>\n<li>In addition, the <b><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a></b> has been managed under the Discovery Program since the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Precursor_Robotic_Program" title="Lunar Precursor Robotic Program">Lunar Precursor Robotic Program</a> was terminated.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals_and_concepts">Proposals and concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Proposals and concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Sample.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="840" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Lunar_Sample.jpg/220px-Lunar_Sample.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Lunar_Sample.jpg/330px-Lunar_Sample.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Lunar_Sample.jpg/440px-Lunar_Sample.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Sample.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Possible configuration of a lunar sample return spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1722" data-file-width="1688" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_in_color_-_Prockter07_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mercury by Discovery\'s <i>MESSENGER</i></div></div></div>\n<p>However often the funding comes in, there is a selection process with perhaps two dozen concepts. These sometimes get further matured and re-proposed in another selection or program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-49">[49]</a></sup> An example of this is <i>Suess-Urey Mission</i>, which was passed over in favor of the successful <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></i> mission, but was eventually flown as <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-49">[49]</a></sup> while a more extensive mission similar to INSIDE was flown as <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">New Frontiers program</a>.  Some of these concepts went on to become actual missions, or similar concepts were eventually realized in another mission class. This list is a mix of previous and current proposals.\n</p><p>Additional examples of Discovery-class mission proposals include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Whipple_(spacecraft)" title="Whipple (spacecraft)">Whipple</a>, space-observatory to detect Oort cloud by transit method</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Io_Volcano_Observer" title="Io Volcano Observer">Io Volcano Observer</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Comet_Hopper" title="Comet Hopper">Comet Hopper</a> (CHopper)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Titan_Mare_Explorer" title="Titan Mare Explorer">Titan Mare Explorer</a> (TiME)</li>\n<li><b>Suess-Urey</b>, similar to the later <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a> mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-49">[49]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Hermes</b>, a Mercury orbiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> (compare to <a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a> Mercury orbiter)</li>\n<li><b>INSIDE Jupiter</b>, an orbiter that would map Jupiter\'s magnetic and gravity fields in an effort to study the giant planet\'s interior structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalists01_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-finalists01-51">[51]</a></sup>  The concept was further matured and implemented as <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">New Frontiers program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space_Missions_Roster_2012_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space_Missions_Roster_2012-52">[52]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <b>Dust Telescope</b> is a space observatory that would measure various properties of incoming <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-dust-53">[53]</a></sup> The dust telescope would combine a trajectory sensor and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">mass spectrometer</a>, to allow the elemental and even isotopic composition to be analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-dust-53">[53]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>OSIRIS</b> (Origins Spectral Interpretation, Resource Identification and Security) was an asteroid observation and sample return mission concept selected in 2006 for further concept studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-select_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-select-54">[54]</a></sup>  It was further matured and launched September 8, 2016 as <a href="/wiki/OSIRIS-REx" title="OSIRIS-REx">OSIRIS-REx</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_Program" title="New Frontiers Program">New Frontiers Program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factsheet_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-factsheet-55">[55]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Hera</b> is a mission concept for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroids" title="Near-Earth asteroids">near-Earth asteroid</a> sample return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Envisioned as the follow-on from the NEAR mission, the design was intended to collect three samples from three different asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hera1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-hera1-57">[57]</a></sup> The initial proposal proposed 1999-AO10, 2000-AG6 and 1998-UQ asteroids, but this was later expanded to three of 40 different options.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hera1_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-hera1-57">[57]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Small Body Grand Tour</b>, an asteroid rendezvous mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bodies_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-bodies-58">[58]</a></sup> This 1993 concept reviews possible targets for what became NEAR&#8212; <a href="/wiki/4660_Nereus" title="4660 Nereus">4660 Nereus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2019_Van_Albada" title="2019 Van Albada">2019 Van Albada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bodies_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-bodies-58">[58]</a></sup> Other targets considered for an extended mission included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encke%27s_comet" title="Encke\'s comet">Encke\'s comet</a> (2P), <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a>, <a href="/wiki/1036_Ganymed" title="1036 Ganymed">1036 Ganymed</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/4015_Wilson%E2%80%93Harrington" title="4015 Wilson&#8211;Harrington">4015 Wilson&#8211;Harrington</a> (1979 VA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bodies_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-bodies-58">[58]</a></sup> (NEAR did visit 433 Eros and Dawn visited 4 Vesta)</li>\n<li><b>Comet Coma Rendezvous Sample Return</b>, a spacecraft designed to rendezvous with a comet, make extended observations within the cometary coma (but not land on the comet), gently collect multiple coma samples, and return them to Earth for study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_SaS_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_SaS-59">[59]</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)"><i>Stardust</i> (spacecraft)</a>)</li>\n<li><b>Micro Exo Explorer</b> would use a new form of micro-electric propulsion, called \'Micro Electro-fluidic-spray Propulsion\' to travel to a near Earth object and gather important data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_focused">Mars focused</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mars focused">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg/220px-PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg/330px-PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg/440px-PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA11858_Starburst_Spider.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mars Geyser Hopper would investigate \'spider\' features on Mars, as imaged by an orbiter. Image size: 1&#160;km (0.62&#160;mi) across.</div></div></div>\n<ul><li><b>Pascal</b>, a Mars climate network mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>MUADEE</b> (Mars Upper Atmosphere Dynamics, Energetics, and Evolution)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delft_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-delft-62">[62]</a></sup> (compare to <a href="/wiki/MAVEN" title="MAVEN">MAVEN</a> of the Mars Scout program)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Phobos_Surveyor" title="Phobos Surveyor">Phobos Surveyor</a> is an orbiter mission concept to the Mars moon Phobos, which would also deploy special rovers for the moon\'s low gravity environment.</li>\n<li>PCROSS, based on <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">LCROSS</a> but to Mars\' moon <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Merlin</b> mission would place a lander on Mars\' moon <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Mars Moons Multiple Landings Mission</b> (M4), would conduct multiple landings on Phobos and Deimos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4363_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_4363-65">[65]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Hall</b> is a Phobos and Deimos sample return mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_1633_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_1633-66">[66]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Aladdin</b> was a Discovery-class Phobos and Deimos sample return mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> It was a finalist in the 1999 Discovery selection, with a planned launch in 2001 and return of the samples by 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sr_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-sr-68">[68]</a></sup> Sample collection was intended to work by sending projectiles into the moons, then collecting the ejecta by means of a collector spacecraft flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sr_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-sr-68">[68]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mars_Geyser_Hopper" title="Mars Geyser Hopper">Mars Geyser Hopper</a> is a lander that would investigate the springtime <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_geyser" title="Martian geyser">Martian geysers</a> found in regions around the <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">south pole of Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geyser_Hopper_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geyser_Hopper-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>MAGIC</b> (Mars Geoscience Imaging at Centimeter-scale) is an orbiter that would provide images of the Martian surface at 5&#8211;10&#160;cm/pixel, permitting resolution of features as small as 20&#8211;40&#160;cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_4325_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_4325-71">[71]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Dragon_(spacecraft)" title="Red Dragon (spacecraft)">Red Dragon</a></i>, a Mars lander and sample return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8m_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-8m-72">[72]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_focused">Lunar focused</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lunar focused">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Lunar sample return from the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin" title="South Pole&#8211;Aitken basin">South Pole&#8211;Aitken basin</a>. No geologic model adequately accounts for all of the characteristics of the area and disagreements are fundamental.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_8017_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_8017-73">[73]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>EXOMOON</b>, <i>in situ</i> investigation on Earth\'s Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>PSOLHO</b>, would use the Moon as an occulter to look for exoplanets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Lunette</b>, a lunar lander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_klaus_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_klaus-76">[76]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Twin Lunar Lander</b>, a geophysics mission to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_2832_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi_2832-77">[77]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vmpmvenus.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="403" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Vmpmvenus.gif/220px-Vmpmvenus.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Vmpmvenus.gif/330px-Vmpmvenus.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Vmpmvenus.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vmpmvenus.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Venus Multiprobe Mission involved sending 16 atmospheric probes into Venus in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus_focused">Venus focused</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Venus focused">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><b>Venus Multiprobe</b>, proposed for a 1999 launch, would have dropped 16 atmospheric probes into Venus, and fall slowly to the surface, making pressure and temperature measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-49">[49]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Vesper</b> was a concept for a Venus orbiter focused on studying that planet\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finalists_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-finalists-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vesper_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-vesper-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> It was one of three concepts to receive funds for further study in the 2006 Discovery selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vesper_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-vesper-80">[80]</a></sup> Osiris and GRAIL were the other two, and eventually GRAIL was chosen and went on to be launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-select_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-select-54">[54]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>V-STAR</b> (Venus Sample Targeting, Attainment and Return) is a Venus atmosphere sample return mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vsr_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-vsr-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trs_0542_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-trs_0542-83">[83]</a></sup> While returning samples from the surface of Venus has noted difficulties, a Discovery-class sample return from the upper atmosphere is being proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vsr_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-vsr-82">[82]</a></sup> Something along the lines of <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust mission</a> but using a free-return trajectory (it would not go into Venusian orbit).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vsr_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-vsr-82">[82]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>VEVA</b> (Venus Exploration of Volcanoes and Atmosphere) is an in atmosphere probe for Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-veva_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-veva-84">[84]</a></sup> The centerpiece is a 7-day balloon flight through the atmosphere accompanied by various tiny probes dropped deeper into the planet\'s thick gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-veva_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-veva-84">[84]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Venus Pathfinder</b>, a long-duration Venus lander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>RAVEN</b>, a Venus orbiter radar mapping mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>VALOR</b>, a Venus mission to study its atmosphere with a balloon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-val_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-val-87">[87]</a></sup> Twin balloons would circumnavigate the planet over 8 Earth-days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-val_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-val-87">[87]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Venus Aircraft</b>, a robotic atmospheric flight on Venus\' atmosphere using a long-duration solar-powered aircraft system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VenusAirplane_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-VenusAirplane-88">[88]</a></sup> It would carry 1.5&#160;kg of scientific payload and it must contend with violent wind, heat and a corrosive atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VenusAirplane_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-VenusAirplane-88">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_Landsailing_Rover" title="Venus Landsailing Rover">Venus Landsailing Rover</a>, a rover concept that would be propelled by the wind force on its vertical wingsail. Conceived in 2012, the project has since made progress in developing electronic components that would allow the vehicle to operate for 50 days on the surface of Venus without a cooling system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zephyr_2015_Report_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zephyr_2015_Report-89">[89]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection_process">Selection process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Selection process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_1_and_2">Discovery 1 and 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Discovery 1 and 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pathfinder01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="649" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Pathfinder01.jpg/220px-Pathfinder01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Pathfinder01.jpg/330px-Pathfinder01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Pathfinder01.jpg/440px-Pathfinder01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pathfinder01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mars Pathfinder\'s <i>Sojourner</i> mini-rover taking its Alpha Particle X-ray Spectrometer measurement of the <a href="/wiki/Yogi_Rock" title="Yogi Rock">Yogi Rock</a> (1997)</div></div></div>\n<p>The first two Discovery missions were <b><a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">Near Earth Asteroid Rendezvous (NEAR)</a></b> (later called Shoemaker NEAR) and <b><a href="/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder" title="Mars Pathfinder">Mars Pathfinder</a></b>. These initial missions did not follow the same selection process that started once the program was under-way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-90">[90]</a></sup> Mars Pathfinder was salvaged from the idea for a technology and EDL demonstrator from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Environmental_Survey" title="Mars Environmental Survey">Mars Environmental Survey</a> program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-90">[90]</a></sup> Also, one of the goals of Pathfinder was to support the Mars Surveyor program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-90">[90]</a></sup> Later missions would be selected by a more sequential process involving Announcements of Opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.nasa.gov_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-mars.nasa.gov-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>NEAR again has a slightly different background: when Discovery was established in 1990, soon after a working group for the program recommended that the first mission should be to a near-Earth asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techdigest.jhuapl.edu_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-techdigest.jhuapl.edu-91">[91]</a></sup> A series of proposals limited to missions to a Near-Earth asteroid missions was reviewed in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techdigest.jhuapl.edu_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-techdigest.jhuapl.edu-91">[91]</a></sup> What would be the NEAR spacecraft mission was formally selected in December 1993, after which a 2-year development period would follow prior to launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techdigest.jhuapl.edu_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-techdigest.jhuapl.edu-91">[91]</a></sup> NEAR was launched on February 15, 1996, and arrived to orbit asteroid Eros on February 14, 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techdigest.jhuapl.edu_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-techdigest.jhuapl.edu-91">[91]</a></sup> Mars Pathfinder launched on December 4, 1996, and would land on the planet Mars on July 4, 1997, bringing along with it the first NASA Mars rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_3_and_4">Discovery 3 and 4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Discovery 3 and 4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg/220px-Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg/330px-Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg/440px-Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Thorium_concentrations.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thorium concentrations on the Moon, as mapped by <i>Lunar Prospector</i></div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1994, NASA made an announcement of opportunity for the next proposed Discovery missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d95_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-d95-93">[93]</a></sup> There were 28 proposals submitted to NASA in October 1994:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d95_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-d95-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ol><li>ASTER- Asteroid Earth Return</li>\n<li>Comet Nucleus Penetrator</li>\n<li>Comet Nucleus Tour (CONTOUR)</li>\n<li>Cometary Coma Chemical Composition (C4)</li>\n<li>Diana  (Lunar and Cometary Mission)</li>\n<li>FRESIP-A mission to Find the Frequency of Earth-sized Inner Planets</li>\n<li>Hermes Global Orbiter (Mercury Orbiter)</li>\n<li>Icy Moon Mission (Lunar Orbiter)</li>\n<li>Interlune-One (Lunar Rovers)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Jovian Integrated Synoptic Telescope (IO Torus investigation)</li>\n<li>Lunar Discovery Orbiter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="background-color: #EEE"><b><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">Lunar Prospector</a></b> (Lunar Orbiter) &#8211; chosen in February 1995 for Discovery 3.</span></li>\n<li>Mainbelt Asteroid Exploration/Rendezvous</li>\n<li>Mars Aerial Platform (Atmospheric)</li>\n<li>Mars Polar Pathfinder (Polar Lander)</li>\n<li>Mars Upper Atmosphere Dynamics, Energetics and Evolution</li>\n<li>Mercury Polar Flyby</li>\n<li>Near Earth Asteroid Returned Sample</li>\n<li>Origin of Asteroids, Comets and Life on Earth</li>\n<li>PELE: A Lunar Mission to Study Planetary Volcanism</li>\n<li>Planetary Research Telescope</li>\n<li>Rendezvous with a Comet Nucleus (RECON)</li>\n<li><span style="background-color: #EEE"><b>Suess-Urey</b> (Solar Wind Sample Return) &#8211; Discovery 4 finalist.</span></li>\n<li>Small Missions to Asteroids and Comets</li>\n<li><span style="background-color: #EEE"><b><a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">Stardust</a></b> (Cometary/Interstellar Dust Return) &#8211; Discovery 4 finalist.</span></li>\n<li>Venus Composition Probe (Atmospheric)</li>\n<li>Venus Environmental Satellite (Atmospheric)</li>\n<li><span style="background-color: #EEE"><b>Venus Multi-Probe Mission</b>(Atmospheric)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> &#8211; Discovery 4 finalist.</span></li></ol>\n</div>\n<p>In February 1995, <b>Lunar Prospector</b>, a lunar orbiter mission, was selected for launch. Three other missions were left to undergo a further selection later in 1995 for the fourth Discovery mission, <i>Stardust</i>, <i>Suess-Urey</i>, and <i>Venus Multiprobe</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d95_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-d95-93">[93]</a></sup> <b>Stardust</b>, a comet sample return mission, was selected in November 1995 over the two other finalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_5_and_6">Discovery 5 and 6</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Discovery 5 and 6">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In October 1997, NASA selected <i><b><a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a></b></i> and <b><a href="/wiki/CONTOUR" title="CONTOUR">CONTOUR</a></b> as the next Discovery missions, out of 34 proposals that were submitted in December 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The five finalists were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Aladdin (Mars\' moon sample return)</li>\n<li>Comet Nucleus Tour (<a href="/wiki/CONTOUR" title="CONTOUR">CONTOUR</a>)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a></i> (Solar wind sample return)</li>\n<li>Mercury Surface, Space Environment, Geochemistry and Ranging mission (<a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a>)</li>\n<li>Venus Environmental Satellite (VESAT)</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_7_and_8">Discovery 7 and 8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Discovery 7 and 8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/220px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/330px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg/440px-Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deep_Impact_HRI.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deep Impact collided with a comet nucleus</div></div></div>\n<p>In July 1999, NASA selected <i><b>MESSENGER</b></i> and <i><b>Deep Impact</b></i> as the next Discovery Program missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov-100">[100]</a></sup> <i>MESSENGER</i> was the first Mercury orbiter and mission to that planet since <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a>, and the <i>Deep Impact</i> sent a projectile into the comet <a href="/wiki/Tempel_1" title="Tempel 1">Tempel 1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov-100">[100]</a></sup> Both missions targeted a launch in late 2004 and the cost was constrained at about US$300 million each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1998 five finalists had been selected to receive US$375,000 to further mature their design concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1998_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1998-101">[101]</a></sup> The five proposals were selected out of perhaps 30 with the goal of achieving the best science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1998_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1998-101">[101]</a></sup> Those missions were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1998_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1998-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Aladdin</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Deep_Impact_(spacecraft)" title="Deep Impact (spacecraft)">Deep Impact</a></i></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MErcury_Surface,_Space_ENvironment,_GEochemistry_and_Ranging" title="MErcury Surface, Space ENvironment, GEochemistry and Ranging">MESSENGER</a></li>\n<li>Inside Jupiter</li>\n<li>Vesper</li></ul>\n<p><i>Aladdin</i> and <i>MESSENGER</i> were also finalists in the 1997 selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1998_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1998-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_9_and_10">Discovery 9 and 10</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Discovery 9 and 10">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Ceres_and_Vesta%2C_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_and_Vesta,_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scale comparison of Vesta, Ceres, and the Moon</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_(crop).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg/220px-Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg/330px-Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg/440px-Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kepler_spacecraft_artist_render_(crop).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Kepler</i> spacecraft art</div></div></div>\n<p>26 proposals were submitted to the 2000 Discovery solicitation, with budget initially targeted at US$300 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-102">[102]</a></sup> Three candidates were shortlisted in January 2001 for a phase-A design study: <i>Dawn, Kepler</i>, and <i>INSIDE Jupiter</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <i>INSIDE Jupiter</i> was similar to a later New Frontiers mission called <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i>; <i>Dawn</i> was a mission to asteroids <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a>, and <i>Kepler</i> was a space telescope mission aimed to discover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a>.
